<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_and_symptoms">Signs and symptoms</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tuberculosis_symptoms.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1250" data-file-width="1410" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Tuberculosis_symptoms.svg/330px-Tuberculosis_symptoms.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Tuberculosis_symptoms.svg/495px-Tuberculosis_symptoms.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Tuberculosis_symptoms.svg/660px-Tuberculosis_symptoms.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tuberculosis_symptoms.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The main symptoms of variants and stages of tuberculosis are given,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> with many symptoms overlapping with other variants, while others are more (but not entirely) specific for certain variants. Multiple variants may be present simultaneously.</div></div></div>
<p>Tuberculosis may infect any part of the body, but most commonly occurs in the lungs (known as pulmonary tuberculosis).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ID10_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-ID10-9">[9]</a></sup> Extrapulmonary TB occurs when tuberculosis develops outside of the lungs, although extrapulmonary TB may coexist with pulmonary TB.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ID10_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-ID10-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>General signs and symptoms include fever, <a href="/wiki/Chills" title="Chills">chills</a>, night sweats, <a href="/wiki/Anorexia_(symptom)" title="Anorexia (symptom)">loss of appetite</a>, weight loss, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatigue_(medical)" title="Fatigue (medical)">fatigue</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ID10_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-ID10-9">[9]</a></sup> Significant <a href="/wiki/Nail_clubbing" title="Nail clubbing">nail clubbing</a> may also occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pet2005_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pet2005-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pulmonary">Pulmonary</span></h3>
<p>If a tuberculosis infection does become active, it most commonly involves the lungs (in about 90% of cases).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Symptoms may include <a href="/wiki/Chest_pain" title="Chest pain">chest pain</a> and a prolonged cough producing sputum. About 25% of people may not have any symptoms (i.e. they remain asymptomatic).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-16">[16]</a></sup> Occasionally, people may <a href="/wiki/Hemoptysis" title="Hemoptysis">cough up blood</a> in small amounts, and in very rare cases, the infection may erode into the <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_artery" title="Pulmonary artery">pulmonary artery</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Rasmussen%27s_aneurysm" title="Rasmussen's aneurysm">Rasmussen's aneurysm</a>, resulting in massive bleeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ID10_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-ID10-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Tuberculosis may become a chronic illness and cause extensive scarring in the upper lobes of the lungs. The upper lung lobes are more frequently affected by tuberculosis than the lower ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ID10_9-5"><a href="#cite_note-ID10-9">[9]</a></sup> The reason for this difference is not clear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robbins_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Robbins-15">[15]</a></sup> It may be due to either better air flow,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robbins_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Robbins-15">[15]</a></sup> or poor <a href="/wiki/Lymph" title="Lymph">lymph</a> drainage within the upper lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ID10_9-6"><a href="#cite_note-ID10-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extrapulmonary">Extrapulmonary</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Extrapulmonary_tuberculosis" title="Extrapulmonary tuberculosis">Extrapulmonary tuberculosis</a></div>
<p>In 15–20% of active cases, the infection spreads outside the lungs, causing other kinds of TB.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> These are collectively denoted as extrapulmonary tuberculosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Extra2005_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Extra2005-22">[22]</a></sup> Extrapulmonary TB occurs more commonly in people with a <a href="/wiki/Immunosuppression" title="Immunosuppression">weakened immune system</a> and young children. In those with HIV, this occurs in more than 50% of cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Extra2005_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Extra2005-22">[22]</a></sup> Notable extrapulmonary infection sites include the <a href="/wiki/Pleural_cavity" title="Pleural cavity">pleura</a> (in tuberculous pleurisy), the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> (in <a href="/wiki/Tuberculous_meningitis" title="Tuberculous meningitis">tuberculous meningitis</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Lymphatic_system" title="Lymphatic system">lymphatic system</a> (in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuberculous_cervical_lymphadenitis" title="Tuberculous cervical lymphadenitis">scrofula</a> of the neck), the <a href="/wiki/Genitourinary_system" title="Genitourinary system">genitourinary system</a> (in <a href="/wiki/Urogenital_tuberculosis" title="Urogenital tuberculosis">urogenital tuberculosis</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/Bone" title="Bone">bones</a> and joints (in <a href="/wiki/Pott_disease" title="Pott disease">Pott disease</a> of the spine), among others. A potentially more serious, widespread form of TB is called "disseminated tuberculosis", it is also known as <a href="/wiki/Miliary_tuberculosis" title="Miliary tuberculosis">miliary tuberculosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ID10_9-7"><a href="#cite_note-ID10-9">[9]</a></sup> Miliary TB currently makes up about 10% of extrapulmonary cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gho2008_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gho2008-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mycobacteria">Mycobacteria</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_tuberculosis" title="Mycobacterium tuberculosis">Mycobacterium tuberculosis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mycobacterium_tuberculosis.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1927" data-file-width="1997" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Mycobacterium_tuberculosis.jpg/220px-Mycobacterium_tuberculosis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Mycobacterium_tuberculosis.jpg/330px-Mycobacterium_tuberculosis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Mycobacterium_tuberculosis.jpg/440px-Mycobacterium_tuberculosis.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mycobacterium_tuberculosis.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scanning_electron_micrograph" title="Scanning electron micrograph">Scanning electron micrograph</a> of <i>M. tuberculosis</i></div></div></div>
<p>The main cause of TB is <i><a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_tuberculosis" title="Mycobacterium tuberculosis">Mycobacterium tuberculosis</a></i> (MTB), a small, <a href="/wiki/Aerobic_organism" title="Aerobic organism">aerobic</a>, nonmotile <a href="/wiki/Bacillus" title="Bacillus">bacillus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ID10_9-8"><a href="#cite_note-ID10-9">[9]</a></sup> The high <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipid</a> content of this <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogen</a> accounts for many of its unique clinical characteristics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> It <a href="/wiki/Cell_division" title="Cell division">divides</a> every 16 to 20 hours, which is an extremely slow rate compared with other bacteria, which usually divide in less than an hour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Mycobacteria have an <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_cell_structure" title="Bacterial cell structure">outer membrane</a> lipid bilayer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Niederweis2010_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Niederweis2010-26">[26]</a></sup> If a <a href="/wiki/Gram_stain" title="Gram stain">Gram stain</a> is performed, MTB either stains very weakly "Gram-positive" or does not retain dye as a result of the high lipid and <a href="/wiki/Mycolic_acid" title="Mycolic acid">mycolic acid</a> content of its cell wall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madison_2001_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Madison_2001-27">[27]</a></sup> MTB can withstand weak <a href="/wiki/Disinfectant" title="Disinfectant">disinfectants</a> and survive in a <a href="/wiki/Endospore" title="Endospore">dry state</a> for weeks. In nature, the bacterium can grow only within the cells of a <a href="/wiki/Host_(biology)" title="Host (biology)">host</a> organism, but <i>M. tuberculosis</i> can be cultured <a href="/wiki/In_vitro" title="In vitro">in the laboratory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parish_1999_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parish_1999-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Using <a href="/wiki/Histology" title="Histology">histological</a> stains on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expectorate" title="Expectorate">expectorated</a> samples from <a href="/wiki/Phlegm" title="Phlegm">phlegm</a> (also called sputum), scientists can identify MTB under a microscope. Since MTB retains certain stains even after being treated with acidic solution, it is classified as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acid-fast_bacillus" title="Acid-fast bacillus">acid-fast bacillus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robbins_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-Robbins-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madison_2001_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Madison_2001-27">[27]</a></sup> The most common acid-fast staining techniques are the <a href="/wiki/Ziehl%E2%80%93Neelsen_stain" title="Ziehl–Neelsen stain">Ziehl–Neelsen stain</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stain2000_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stain2000-29">[29]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Kinyoun_stain" title="Kinyoun stain">Kinyoun stain</a>, which dye acid-fast bacilli a bright red that stands out against a blue background.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auramine-rhodamine_stain" title="Auramine-rhodamine stain">Auramine-rhodamine staining</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kommareddi_1984_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kommareddi_1984-31">[31]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Fluorescence_microscope" title="Fluorescence microscope">fluorescence microscopy</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> are also used.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_tuberculosis_complex" title="Mycobacterium tuberculosis complex"><i>M. tuberculosis</i> complex</a> (MTBC) includes four other TB-causing <a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium" title="Mycobacterium">mycobacteria</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_bovis" title="Mycobacterium bovis">M. bovis</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_africanum" title="Mycobacterium africanum">M. africanum</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_canetti" title="Mycobacterium canetti">M. canetti</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_microti" title="Mycobacterium microti">M. microti</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> <i>M. africanum</i> is not widespread, but it is a significant cause of tuberculosis in parts of Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> <i>M. bovis</i> was once a common cause of tuberculosis, but the introduction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pasteurisation" title="Pasteurisation">pasteurized milk</a> has almost eliminated this as a public health problem in developed countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robbins_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-Robbins-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> <i>M. canetti</i> is rare and seems to be limited to the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>, although a few cases have been seen in African emigrants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> <i>M. microti</i> is also rare and is seen almost only in immunodeficient people, although its <a href="/wiki/Prevalence" title="Prevalence">prevalence</a> may be significantly underestimated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Other known pathogenic mycobacteria include <i><a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_leprae" title="Mycobacterium leprae">M. leprae</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_avium_complex" title="Mycobacterium avium complex">M. avium</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_kansasii" title="Mycobacterium kansasii">M. kansasii</a></i>. The latter two species are classified as "<a href="/wiki/Nontuberculous_mycobacteria" title="Nontuberculous mycobacteria">nontuberculous mycobacteria</a>" (NTM) or atypical mycobacteria. NTM cause neither TB nor <a href="/wiki/Leprosy" title="Leprosy">leprosy</a>, but they do cause lung diseases that resemble TB.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ALA_1997_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-ALA_1997-40">[40]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TB_poster.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1389" data-file-width="904" decoding="async" height="338" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/TB_poster.jpg/220px-TB_poster.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/TB_poster.jpg/330px-TB_poster.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/TB_poster.jpg/440px-TB_poster.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TB_poster.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Public health campaigns in the 1920s tried to halt the spread of TB.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transmission">Transmission</span></h3>
<p>When people with active pulmonary TB cough, sneeze, speak, sing, or spit, they expel infectious <a href="/wiki/Aerosol" title="Aerosol">aerosol</a> droplets 0.5 to 5.0 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%9Cm" title="Μm">µm</a> in diameter. A single sneeze can release up to 40,000 droplets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cole_1998_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cole_1998-41">[41]</a></sup> Each one of these droplets may transmit the disease, since the infectious dose of tuberculosis is very small (the inhalation of fewer than 10 bacteria may cause an infection).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Risk_of_transmission">Risk of transmission</span></h4>
<p>People with prolonged, frequent, or close contact with people with TB are at particularly high risk of becoming infected, with an estimated 22% infection rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ahmed_2011_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmed_2011-43">[43]</a></sup> A person with active but untreated tuberculosis may infect 10–15 (or more) other people per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2012data_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2012data-44">[44]</a></sup> Transmission should occur from only people with active TB – those with latent infection are not thought to be contagious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robbins_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-Robbins-15">[15]</a></sup> The probability of transmission from one person to another depends upon several factors, including the number of infectious droplets expelled by the carrier, the effectiveness of ventilation, the duration of exposure, the <a href="/wiki/Virulence" title="Virulence">virulence</a> of the <i>M. tuberculosis</i> <a href="/wiki/Strain_(biology)" title="Strain (biology)">strain</a>, the level of immunity in the uninfected person, and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDCcourse_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDCcourse-45">[45]</a></sup> The cascade of person-to-person spread can be circumvented by segregating those with active ("overt") TB and putting them on anti-TB drug regimens. After about two weeks of effective treatment, subjects with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistance" title="Antibiotic resistance">nonresistant</a> active infections generally do not remain contagious to others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ahmed_2011_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmed_2011-43">[43]</a></sup> If someone does become infected, it typically takes three to four weeks before the newly infected person becomes infectious enough to transmit the disease to others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Risk_factors">Risk factors</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Risk_factors_for_tuberculosis" title="Risk factors for tuberculosis">Risk factors for tuberculosis</a></div>
<p>A number of factors make individuals more susceptible to TB infection and/or disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Active_disease_risk">Active disease risk</span></h4>
<p>The most important risk factor globally for developing active TB is concurrent HIV infection; 13% of those with TB are also infected with HIV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-48">[48]</a></sup> This is a particular problem in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>, where HIV infection rates are high.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Of those without HIV infection who are infected with tuberculosis, about 5–10% develop active disease during their lifetimes;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pet2005_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pet2005-18">[18]</a></sup> in contrast, 30% of those co-infected with HIV develop the active disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pet2005_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pet2005-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Use of certain medications, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corticosteroids" title="Corticosteroids">corticosteroids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Infliximab" title="Infliximab">infliximab</a> (an anti-αTNF monoclonal antibody), is another important risk factor, especially in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developed_world" title="Developed world">developed world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other risk factors include: <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism" title="Alcoholism">alcoholism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-16">[16]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus" title="Diabetes mellitus">diabetes mellitus</a> (3-fold increased risk),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Silicosis" title="Silicosis">silicosis</a> (30-fold increased risk),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-table3_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-table3-52">[52]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cigarette" title="Cigarette">tobacco smoking</a> (2-fold increased risk),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Indoor_air_quality" title="Indoor air quality">indoor air pollution</a>, malnutrition, young age,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-47">[47]</a></sup> recently acquired TB infection, recreational drug use, severe kidney disease, low body weight, organ transplant, head and neck cancer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetic_susceptibility" title="Genetic susceptibility">genetic susceptibility</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> (the overall importance of genetic risk factors remains undefined<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_16-5"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-16">[16]</a></sup>).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Infection_susceptibility">Infection susceptibility</span></h4>
<p>Tobacco smoking increases the risk of infections (in addition to increasing the risk of active disease and death). Additional factors increasing infection susceptibility include young age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathogenesis">Pathogenesis</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carswell-Tubercle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="644" data-file-width="479" decoding="async" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Carswell-Tubercle.jpg/170px-Carswell-Tubercle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Carswell-Tubercle.jpg/255px-Carswell-Tubercle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Carswell-Tubercle.jpg/340px-Carswell-Tubercle.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carswell-Tubercle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Robert_Carswell_(pathologist)" title="Robert Carswell (pathologist)">Robert Carswell</a>'s illustration of tubercle<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoodCooper1835_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-GoodCooper1835-56">[56]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>About 90% of those infected with <i>M. tuberculosis</i> have <a href="/wiki/Asymptomatic" title="Asymptomatic">asymptomatic</a>, latent TB infections (sometimes called LTBI),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book90_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Book90-57">[57]</a></sup> with only a 10% lifetime chance that the latent infection will progress to overt, active tuberculous disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arch2009_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arch2009-58">[58]</a></sup> In those with HIV, the risk of developing active TB increases to nearly 10% a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arch2009_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Arch2009-58">[58]</a></sup> If effective treatment is not given, the death rate for active TB cases is up to 66%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2012data_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2012data-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tuberculous_epididymitis_Low_Power.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Tuberculous_epididymitis_Low_Power.jpg/220px-Tuberculous_epididymitis_Low_Power.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Tuberculous_epididymitis_Low_Power.jpg/330px-Tuberculous_epididymitis_Low_Power.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Tuberculous_epididymitis_Low_Power.jpg/440px-Tuberculous_epididymitis_Low_Power.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tuberculous_epididymitis_Low_Power.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Microscopy of tuberculous epididymitis. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H%26E" title="H&amp;E">H&amp;E</a> stain</div></div></div>
<p>TB infection begins when the mycobacteria reach the <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_alveolus" title="Pulmonary alveolus">alveolar air sacs</a> of the lungs, where they invade and replicate within <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endosomes" title="Endosomes">endosomes</a> of alveolar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macrophages" title="Macrophages">macrophages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robbins_15-6"><a href="#cite_note-Robbins-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Houben_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Houben-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Macrophages identify the bacterium as foreign and attempt to eliminate it by <a href="/wiki/Phagocytosis" title="Phagocytosis">phagocytosis</a>. During this process, the bacterium is enveloped by the macrophage and stored temporarily in a membrane-bound vesicle called a phagosome. The phagosome then combines with a lysosome to create a phagolysosome. In the phagolysosome, the cell attempts to use <a href="/wiki/Reactive_oxygen_species" title="Reactive oxygen species">reactive oxygen species</a> and acid to kill the bacterium.  However, <i>M. tuberculosis</i> has a thick, waxy <a href="/wiki/Mycolic_acid" title="Mycolic acid">mycolic acid</a> capsule that protects it from these toxic substances. <i>M. tuberculosis</i> is able to reproduce inside the macrophage and will eventually kill the immune cell.
</p><p>The primary site of infection in the lungs, known as the <a href="/wiki/Ghon_focus" title="Ghon focus">Ghon focus</a>, is generally located in either the upper part of the lower lobe, or the lower part of the <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">upper lobe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robbins_15-7"><a href="#cite_note-Robbins-15">[15]</a></sup> Tuberculosis of the lungs may also occur via infection from the blood stream. This is known as a <a href="/wiki/Simon_focus" title="Simon focus">Simon focus</a> and is typically found in the top of the lung.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> This hematogenous transmission can also spread infection to more distant sites, such as peripheral lymph nodes, the kidneys, the brain, and the bones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robbins_15-8"><a href="#cite_note-Robbins-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herrmann_2005_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Herrmann_2005-62">[62]</a></sup> All parts of the body can be affected by the disease, though for unknown reasons it rarely affects the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skeletal_muscle" title="Skeletal muscle">skeletal muscles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pancreas" title="Pancreas">pancreas</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Thyroid" title="Thyroid">thyroid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tuberculosis is classified as one of the <a href="/wiki/Granuloma" title="Granuloma">granulomatous</a> inflammatory diseases. <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">Macrophages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epithelioid_cell" title="Epithelioid cell">epithelioid cells</a>, <a href="/wiki/T_cell" title="T cell">T lymphocytes</a>, <a href="/wiki/B_cell" title="B cell">B lymphocytes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fibroblast" title="Fibroblast">fibroblasts</a>  aggregate to form granulomas, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lymphocytes" title="Lymphocytes">lymphocytes</a> surrounding the infected macrophages. When other macrophages attack the infected macrophage, they fuse together to form a giant multinucleated cell in the alveolar lumen. The granuloma may prevent dissemination of the mycobacteria and provide a local environment for interaction of cells of the immune system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grosset_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grosset-64">[64]</a></sup> However, more recent evidence suggests that the bacteria use the granulomas to avoid destruction by the host's immune system.  Macrophages and <a href="/wiki/Dendritic_cell" title="Dendritic cell">dendritic cells</a> in the granulomas are unable to present antigen to lymphocytes; thus the immune response is suppressed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Bacteria inside the granuloma can become dormant, resulting in latent infection. Another feature of the granulomas is the development of abnormal cell death (<a href="/wiki/Necrosis" title="Necrosis">necrosis</a>) in the center of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tubercle_(anatomy)" title="Tubercle (anatomy)">tubercles</a>.  To the naked eye, this has the texture of soft, white cheese and is termed <a href="/wiki/Caseous_necrosis" title="Caseous necrosis">caseous necrosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grosset_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grosset-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>If TB bacteria gain entry to the blood stream from an area of damaged tissue, they can spread throughout the body and set up many foci of infection, all appearing as tiny, white tubercles in the tissues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> This severe form of TB disease, most common in young children and those with HIV, is called miliary tuberculosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> People with this disseminated TB have a high fatality rate even with treatment (about 30%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gho2008_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gho2008-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>In many people, the infection waxes and wanes. Tissue destruction and necrosis are often balanced by healing and <a href="/wiki/Fibrosis" title="Fibrosis">fibrosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grosset_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-Grosset-64">[64]</a></sup> Affected tissue is replaced by scarring and cavities filled with caseous necrotic material. During active disease, some of these cavities are joined to the air passages (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bronchi" title="Bronchi">bronchi</a>) and this material can be coughed up. It contains living bacteria and thus can spread the infection. Treatment with appropriate <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a> kills bacteria and allows healing to take place. Upon cure, affected areas are eventually replaced by scar tissue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grosset_64-3"><a href="#cite_note-Grosset-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis_diagnosis" title="Tuberculosis diagnosis">Tuberculosis diagnosis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TB_in_sputum.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2047" data-file-width="2047" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/TB_in_sputum.png/220px-TB_in_sputum.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/TB_in_sputum.png/330px-TB_in_sputum.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/TB_in_sputum.png/440px-TB_in_sputum.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TB_in_sputum.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>M. tuberculosis</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ziehl-Neelsen_stain" title="Ziehl-Neelsen stain">stained red</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Sputum" title="Sputum">sputum</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Active_tuberculosis">Active tuberculosis</span></h3>
<p>Diagnosing active tuberculosis based only on signs and symptoms is difficult,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DiagP2011_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-DiagP2011-69">[69]</a></sup> as is diagnosing the disease in those who have a weakened immune system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clinic2009_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clinic2009-70">[70]</a></sup> A diagnosis of TB should, however, be considered in those with signs of lung disease or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitutional_symptoms" title="Constitutional symptoms">constitutional symptoms</a> lasting longer than two weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clinic2009_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clinic2009-70">[70]</a></sup> A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chest_X-ray" title="Chest X-ray">chest X-ray</a> and multiple <a href="/wiki/Sputum_culture" title="Sputum culture">sputum cultures</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acid-fast_bacilli" title="Acid-fast bacilli">acid-fast bacilli</a> are typically part of the initial evaluation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clinic2009_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-Clinic2009-70">[70]</a></sup> Interferon-γ release assays and tuberculin skin tests are of little use in most of the developing world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sester_100–11_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sester_100–11-72">[72]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Interferon_gamma_release_assay" title="Interferon gamma release assay">Interferon gamma release assays</a> (IGRA) have similar limitations in those with HIV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sester_100–11_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sester_100–11-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>A definitive diagnosis of TB is made by identifying <i>M. tuberculosis</i> in a clinical sample (e.g., sputum, <a href="/wiki/Pus" title="Pus">pus</a>, or a <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">tissue</a> <a href="/wiki/Biopsy" title="Biopsy">biopsy</a>). However, the difficult culture process for this slow-growing organism can take two to six weeks for blood or sputum culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Thus, treatment is often begun before cultures are confirmed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICE2011_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-NICE2011-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid_amplification_test" title="Nucleic acid amplification test">Nucleic acid amplification tests</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_deaminase" title="Adenosine deaminase">adenosine deaminase</a> testing may allow rapid diagnosis of TB.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DiagP2011_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-DiagP2011-69">[69]</a></sup> Blood tests to detect antibodies are not <a href="/wiki/Sensitivity_and_specificity" title="Sensitivity and specificity">specific or sensitive</a>, so they are not recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latent_tuberculosis">Latent tuberculosis</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Latent_tuberculosis" title="Latent tuberculosis">Latent tuberculosis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mantoux_tuberculin_skin_test.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1312" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Mantoux_tuberculin_skin_test.jpg/220px-Mantoux_tuberculin_skin_test.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Mantoux_tuberculin_skin_test.jpg/330px-Mantoux_tuberculin_skin_test.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Mantoux_tuberculin_skin_test.jpg/440px-Mantoux_tuberculin_skin_test.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mantoux_tuberculin_skin_test.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mantoux_test" title="Mantoux test">Mantoux tuberculin skin test</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mantoux_test" title="Mantoux test">Mantoux tuberculin skin test</a> is often used to screen people at high risk for TB.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clinic2009_70-3"><a href="#cite_note-Clinic2009-70">[70]</a></sup> Those who have been previously immunized with the Bacille Calmette-Guerin vaccine may have a false-positive test result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothel_2005_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rothel_2005-77">[77]</a></sup> The test may be falsely negative in those with <a href="/wiki/Sarcoidosis" title="Sarcoidosis">sarcoidosis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hodgkin%27s_lymphoma" title="Hodgkin's lymphoma">Hodgkin's lymphoma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a>, and most notably, active tuberculosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robbins_15-9"><a href="#cite_note-Robbins-15">[15]</a></sup> Interferon gamma release assays, on a blood sample, are recommended in those who are positive to the Mantoux test.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICE2011_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-NICE2011-75">[75]</a></sup> These are not affected by immunization or most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_mycobacteria" title="Environmental mycobacteria">environmental mycobacteria</a>, so they generate fewer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/False-positive" title="False-positive">false-positive</a> results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> However, they are affected by <i>M. szulgai</i>, <i>M. marinum</i>, and <i>M. kansasii</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> IGRAs may increase sensitivity when used in addition to the skin test, but may be less sensitive than the skin test when used alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Preventive_Services_Task_Force" title="US Preventive Services Task Force">US Preventive Services Task Force</a> (USPSTF) has recommended screening people who are at high risk for latent tuberculosis with either tuberculin skin tests or interferon-gamma release assays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> While some have recommend testing health care workers, evidence of benefit for this is poor as of 2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tuberculosis&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a> (CDC) stopped recommending yearly testing of health care workers without known exposure in 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Notice_Do_not_spit_-_National_Association_for_the_Prevention_of_Tuberculosis_Dublin_Branch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2741" data-file-width="2078" decoding="async" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Notice_Do_not_spit_-_National_Association_for_the_Prevention_of_Tuberculosis_Dublin_Branch.jpg/220px-Notice_Do_not_spit_-_National_Association_for_the_Prevention_of_Tuberculosis_Dublin_Branch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Notice_Do_not_spit_-_National_Association_for_the_Prevention_of_Tuberculosis_Dublin_Branch.jpg/330px-Notice_Do_not_spit_-_National_Association_for_the_Prevention_of_Tuberculosis_Dublin_Branch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Notice_Do_not_spit_-_National_Association_for_the_Prevention_of_Tuberculosis_Dublin_Branch.jpg/440px-Notice_Do_not_spit_-_National_Association_for_the_Prevention_of_Tuberculosis_Dublin_Branch.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Notice_Do_not_spit_-_National_Association_for_the_Prevention_of_Tuberculosis_Dublin_Branch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tuberculosis public health campaign in Ireland, c. 1905</div></div></div>
<p>Tuberculosis prevention and control efforts rely primarily on the vaccination of infants and the detection and appropriate treatment of active cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_16-6"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-16">[16]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) has achieved some success with improved treatment regimens, and a small decrease in case numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_16-7"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-16">[16]</a></sup> Some countries have legislation to involuntarily detain or examine those suspected to have tuberculosis, or <a href="/wiki/Involuntary_treatment" title="Involuntary treatment">involuntarily treat</a> them if infected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vaccines">Vaccines</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis_vaccines" title="Tuberculosis vaccines">Tuberculosis vaccines</a> and <a href="/wiki/BCG_vaccine" title="BCG vaccine">BCG vaccine</a></div>
<p>The only available <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccine</a> as of 2021<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tuberculosis&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacillus_Calmette-Gu%C3%A9rin" title="Bacillus Calmette-Guérin">bacillus Calmette-Guérin</a> (BCG).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McS2011_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-McS2011-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> In children it decreases the risk of getting the infection by 20% and the risk of infection turning into active disease by nearly 60%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is the most widely used vaccine worldwide, with more than 90% of all children being vaccinated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_16-8"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-16">[16]</a></sup> The immunity it induces decreases after about ten years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_16-9"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-16">[16]</a></sup> As tuberculosis is uncommon in most of Canada, Western Europe, and the United States, BCG is administered to only those people at high risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UK06_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-UK06-90">[90]</a></sup> Part of the reasoning against the use of the vaccine is that it makes the tuberculin skin test falsely positive, reducing the test's usefulness as a screening tool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UK06_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-UK06-90">[90]</a></sup> Several vaccines are being developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_16-10"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Intradermal MVA85A vaccine in addition to BCG injection is not effective in preventing tuberculosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_health">Public health</span></h3>
<p>Public health campaigns which have focused on overcrowding, public spitting and regular sanitation (including hand washing) during the 1800s helped to either interrupt or slow spread which when combined with contact tracing, isolation and treatment helped to dramatically curb the transmission of both tuberculosis and other airborne diseases which led to the <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis_elimination" title="Tuberculosis elimination">elimination of tuberculosis</a> as a major public health issue in most developed economies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Other risk factors which worsened TB spread such as malnutrition were also ameliorated, but since the emergence of HIV a new population of immunocompromised individuals was available for TB to infect.
</p><p>The World Health Organization (WHO) declared TB a "global health emergency" in 1993,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_16-11"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-16">[16]</a></sup> and in 2006, the Stop TB Partnership developed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_Plan_to_Stop_Tuberculosis" title="Global Plan to Stop Tuberculosis">Global Plan to Stop Tuberculosis</a> that aimed to save 14 million lives between its launch and 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> A number of targets they set were not achieved by 2015, mostly due to the increase in HIV-associated tuberculosis and the emergence of multiple drug-resistant tuberculosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_16-12"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-16">[16]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis_classification" title="Tuberculosis classification">tuberculosis classification</a> system developed by the <a href="/wiki/American_Thoracic_Society" title="American Thoracic Society">American Thoracic Society</a> is used primarily in public health programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> In 2015, it launched the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=End_TB_Strategy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="End TB Strategy (page does not exist)">End TB Strategy</a> to reduce deaths by 95% and incidence by 90% before 2035. The goal of tuberculosis elimination is hampered by the lack of rapid testing, of short and effective treatment courses, and of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuberculosis_vaccine" title="Tuberculosis vaccine">completely effective vaccines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>The benefits and risks of giving anti-tubercular drugs in those exposed to MDR-TB is unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Making HAART therapy available to HIV-positive individuals significantly reduces the risk of progression to an active TB infection by up to 90% and can mitigate the spread through this population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment">Treatment</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis_management" title="Tuberculosis management">Tuberculosis management</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tubi_-_1234,0186.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="366" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Tubi_-_1234%2C0186.jpg/220px-Tubi_-_1234%2C0186.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Tubi_-_1234%2C0186.jpg/330px-Tubi_-_1234%2C0186.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Tubi_-_1234%2C0186.jpg/440px-Tubi_-_1234%2C0186.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tubi_-_1234,0186.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tuberculosis phototherapy treatment on 3 March 1934, in <a href="/wiki/Kuopio" title="Kuopio">Kuopio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a></div></div></div>
<p>Treatment of TB uses antibiotics to kill the bacteria. Effective TB treatment is difficult, due to the unusual structure and chemical composition of the mycobacterial cell wall, which hinders the entry of drugs and makes many antibiotics ineffective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>Active TB is best treated with combinations of several antibiotics to reduce the risk of the bacteria developing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistance" title="Antibiotic resistance">antibiotic resistance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_16-13"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-16">[16]</a></sup> The routine use of <a href="/wiki/Rifabutin" title="Rifabutin">rifabutin</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Rifampicin" title="Rifampicin">rifampicin</a> in HIV-positive people with tuberculosis is of unclear benefit as of 2007<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tuberculosis&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latent_TB">Latent TB</span></h3>
<p>Latent TB is treated with either <a href="/wiki/Isoniazid" title="Isoniazid">isoniazid</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rifampin" title="Rifampin">rifampin</a> alone, or a combination of isoniazid with either rifampicin or rifapentine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOlatent2018_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHOlatent2018-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ste2020_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ste2020-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>The treatment takes three to nine months depending on the medications used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDCcourse_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDCcourse-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOlatent2018_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHOlatent2018-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ste2020_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ste2020-103">[103]</a></sup> People with latent infections are treated to prevent them from progressing to active TB disease later in life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Latent2011_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Latent2011-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Education or counselling may improve the latent tuberculosis treatment completion rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_onset">New onset</span></h3>
<p>The recommended treatment of new-onset pulmonary tuberculosis, as of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tuberculosis&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, is six months of a combination of antibiotics containing rifampicin, isoniazid, <a href="/wiki/Pyrazinamide" title="Pyrazinamide">pyrazinamide</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ethambutol" title="Ethambutol">ethambutol</a> for the first two months, and only rifampicin and isoniazid for the last four months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_16-14"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-16">[16]</a></sup> Where resistance to isoniazid is high, ethambutol may be added for the last four months as an alternative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_16-15"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-16">[16]</a></sup> Treatment with anti-TB drugs for at least 6 months results in higher success rates when compared with treatment less than 6 months, even though the difference is small. Shorter treatment regimen may be recommended for those with compliance issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gelband_1999_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gelband_1999-107">[107]</a></sup> There is also no evidence to support shorter anti-tuberculosis treatment regimens when compared to a 6-month treatment regimen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> However recently, results from an international, randomized, controlled clinical trial indicate that a four-month daily treatment regimen containing high-dose, or "optimized," rifapentine with moxifloxacin (2PHZM/2PHM) is as safe and effective as the existing standard six-month daily regimen at curing drug-susceptible tuberculosis (TB) disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recurrent_disease">Recurrent disease</span></h3>
<p>If tuberculosis recurs, testing to determine which antibiotics it is sensitive to is important before determining treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_16-16"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-16">[16]</a></sup> If <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multidrug-resistant_TB" title="Multidrug-resistant TB">multiple drug-resistant TB</a> (MDR-TB) is detected, treatment with at least four effective antibiotics for 18 to 24 months is recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_16-17"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medication_administration">Medication administration</span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Directly_observed_therapy" title="Directly observed therapy">Directly observed therapy</a>, i.e., having a health care provider watch the person take their medications, is recommended by the World Health Organization (WHO) in an effort to reduce the number of people not appropriately taking antibiotics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> The evidence to support this practice over people simply taking their medications independently is of poor quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karumbi2015_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karumbi2015-111">[111]</a></sup> There is no strong evidence indicating that directly observed therapy improves the number of people who were cured or the number of people who complete their medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karumbi2015_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-Karumbi2015-111">[111]</a></sup> Moderate quality evidence suggests that there is also no difference if people are observed at home versus at a clinic, or by a family member versus a health care worker.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karumbi2015_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-Karumbi2015-111">[111]</a></sup> Methods to remind people of the importance of treatment and appointments may result in a small but important improvement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> There is also not enough evidence to support intermittent rifampicin-containing therapy given two to three times a week has equal effectiveness as daily dose regimen on improving cure rates and reducing relapsing rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> There is also not enough evidence on effectiveness of giving intermittent twice or thrice weekly short course regimen compared to daily dosing regimen in treating children with tuberculosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medication_resistance">Medication resistance</span></h3>
<p>Primary resistance occurs when a person becomes infected with a resistant strain of TB. A person with fully susceptible <a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_tuberculosis" title="Mycobacterium tuberculosis">MTB</a> may develop secondary (acquired) resistance during therapy because of inadequate treatment, not taking the prescribed regimen appropriately (lack of compliance), or using low-quality medication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OBrien_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-OBrien-115">[115]</a></sup> Drug-resistant TB is a serious public health issue in many developing countries, as its treatment is longer and requires more expensive drugs. MDR-TB is defined as resistance to the two most effective first-line TB drugs: rifampicin and isoniazid. Extensively drug-resistant TB is also resistant to three or more of the six classes of second-line drugs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMWR2006_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-MMWR2006-116">[116]</a></sup> Totally drug-resistant TB is resistant to all currently used drugs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TR2012_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-TR2012-117">[117]</a></sup> It was first observed in 2003 in Italy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> but not widely reported until 2012,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TR2012_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-TR2012-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> and has also been found in Iran and India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EIU_2014_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-EIU_2014-120">[120]</a></sup> There is some efficacy for linezolid to treat those with XDR-TB but side effects and discontinuation of medications were common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bedaquiline" title="Bedaquiline">Bedaquiline</a> is tentatively supported for use in multiple drug-resistant TB.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>XDR-TB is a term sometimes used to define <i>extensively resistant</i> TB, and constitutes one in ten cases of MDR-TB. Cases of XDR TB have been identified in more than 90% of countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EIU_2014_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-EIU_2014-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>For those with known rifampicin or MDR-TB, molecular tests such as the Genotype® MTBDRsl Assay (performed on culture isolates or smear positive specimens) may be useful to detect second-line anti-tubercular drug resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prognosis">Prognosis</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tuberculosis_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="940" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Tuberculosis_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/310px-Tuberculosis_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Tuberculosis_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/465px-Tuberculosis_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Tuberculosis_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/620px-Tuberculosis_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tuberculosis_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Age_adjustment" title="Age adjustment">Age-standardized</a> <a href="/wiki/Disability-adjusted_life_year" title="Disability-adjusted life year">disability-adjusted life years</a> caused by tuberculosis per 100,000 inhabitants in 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> <div> <table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; padding: 0; border: 0; background:transparent; width:100%;"> <tbody><tr> <td style="text-align: left; vertical-align: top;"> <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#b3b3b3; color:black;font-size:60%;"> </span> no data</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffff65; color:black;font-size:60%;"> </span> ≤10</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#fff200; color:black;font-size:60%;"> </span> 10–25</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffdc00; color:black;font-size:60%;"> </span> 25–50</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffc600; color:black;font-size:60%;"> </span> 50–75</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffb000; color:black;font-size:60%;"> </span> 75–100</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff9a00; color:black;font-size:60%;"> </span> 100–250</div> </td> <td style="text-align: left; vertical-align: top;"> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff8400; color:black;font-size:60%;"> </span> 250–500</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff6e00; color:black;font-size:60%;"> </span> 500–750</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff5800; color:black;font-size:60%;"> </span> 750–1000</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff4200; color:black;font-size:60%;"> </span> 1000–2000</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff2c00; color:black;font-size:60%;"> </span> 2000–3000</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#cb0000; color:white;font-size:60%;"> </span> ≥ 3000</div> </td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div></div>
<p>Progression from TB infection to overt TB disease occurs when the bacilli overcome the immune system defenses and begin to multiply. In primary TB disease (some 1–5% of cases), this occurs soon after the initial infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robbins_15-10"><a href="#cite_note-Robbins-15">[15]</a></sup> However, in the majority of cases, a <a href="/wiki/Latent_tuberculosis" title="Latent tuberculosis">latent infection</a> occurs with no obvious symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robbins_15-11"><a href="#cite_note-Robbins-15">[15]</a></sup> These dormant bacilli produce active tuberculosis in 5–10% of these latent cases, often many years after infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pet2005_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-Pet2005-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>The risk of reactivation increases with <a href="/wiki/Immunosuppression" title="Immunosuppression">immunosuppression</a>, such as that caused by infection with HIV. In people coinfected with <i>M. tuberculosis</i> and HIV, the risk of reactivation increases to 10% per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robbins_15-12"><a href="#cite_note-Robbins-15">[15]</a></sup> Studies using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DNA_fingerprinting" title="DNA fingerprinting">DNA fingerprinting</a> of <i>M. tuberculosis</i> strains have shown reinfection contributes more substantially to recurrent TB than previously thought,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> with estimates that it might account for more than 50% of reactivated cases in areas where TB is common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> The chance of death from a case of tuberculosis is about 4% as of 2008<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tuberculosis&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, down from 8% in 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_16-18"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>In people with smear-positive pulmonary TB (without HIV co-infection), after 5 years without treatment, 50-60% die while 20-25% achieve spontaneous resolution (cure). TB is almost always fatal in those with untreated HIV co-infection and death rates are increased even with antiretroviral treatment of HIV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span></h2>
<p>Roughly one-quarter of the world's population has been infected with <i>M. tuberculosis</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2018Fact_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2018Fact-6">[6]</a></sup> with new infections occurring in about 1% of the population each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2002_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2002-12">[12]</a></sup> However, most infections with <i>M. tuberculosis</i> do not cause disease,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC-130">[130]</a></sup> and 90–95% of infections remain asymptomatic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book90_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Book90-57">[57]</a></sup> In 2012, an estimated 8.6 million chronic cases were active.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> In 2010, 8.8 million new cases of tuberculosis were diagnosed, and 1.20–1.45 million deaths occurred (most of these occurring in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_nation" title="Developing nation">developing countries</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loz2012_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Loz2012-132">[132]</a></sup> Of these, about 0.35 million occur in those also infected with HIV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011Control_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011Control-133">[133]</a></sup> In 2018, tuberculosis was the leading cause of death worldwide from a single infectious agent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> The total number of tuberculosis cases has been decreasing since 2005, while new cases have decreased since 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tuberculosis incidence is seasonal, with peaks occurring every spring and summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Korthals2012_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Korthals2012-138">[138]</a></sup> The reasons for this are unclear, but may be related to vitamin D deficiency during the winter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Korthals2012_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-Korthals2012-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> There are also studies linking tuberculosis to different weather conditions like low temperature, low humidity and low rainfall. It has been suggested that tuberculosis incidence rates may be connected to climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="At-risk_groups">At-risk groups</span></h3>
<p>Tuberculosis is closely linked to both overcrowding and <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a>, making it one of the principal <a href="/wiki/Diseases_of_poverty" title="Diseases of poverty">diseases of poverty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_16-19"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-16">[16]</a></sup> Those at high risk thus include: people who inject illicit drugs, inhabitants and employees of locales where vulnerable people gather (e.g., prisons and homeless shelters), medically underprivileged and resource-poor communities, high-risk ethnic minorities, children in close contact with high-risk category patients, and health-care providers serving these patients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Griffith_1996_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Griffith_1996-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>The rate of tuberculosis varies with age. In Africa, it primarily affects adolescents and young adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> However, in countries where incidence rates have declined dramatically (such as the United States), tuberculosis is mainly a disease of the elderly and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunocompromise" title="Immunocompromise">immunocompromised</a> (risk factors are listed above).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robbins_15-13"><a href="#cite_note-Robbins-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> Worldwide, 22 "high-burden" states or countries together experience 80% of cases as well as 83% of deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EIU_2014_120-2"><a href="#cite_note-EIU_2014-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Canada and Australia, tuberculosis is many times more common among the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aboriginal_peoples" title="Aboriginal peoples">aboriginal peoples</a>, especially in remote areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Factors contributing to this include higher prevalence of predisposing health conditions and behaviours, and overcrowding and poverty. In some Canadian aboriginal groups, genetic susceptibility may play a role.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_47-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Socioeconomic status (SES) strongly affects TB risk. People of low SES are both more likely to contract TB and to be more severely affected by the disease. Those with low SES are more likely to be affected by risk factors for developing TB (e.g. malnutrition, indoor air pollution, HIV co-infection, etc.), and are additionally more likely to be exposed to crowded and poorly ventilated spaces. Inadequate healthcare also means that people with active disease who facilitate spread are not diagnosed and treated promptly; sick people thus remain in the infectious state and (continue to) spread the infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_47-4"><a href="#cite_note-:0-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographical_epidemiology">Geographical epidemiology</span></h3>
<p>The distribution of tuberculosis is not uniform across the globe; about 80% of the population in many African, Caribbean, South Asian, and eastern European countries test positive in tuberculin tests, while only 5–10% of the U.S. population test positive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robbins_15-14"><a href="#cite_note-Robbins-15">[15]</a></sup> Hopes of totally controlling the disease have been dramatically dampened because of many factors, including the difficulty of developing an effective vaccine, the expensive and time-consuming diagnostic process, the necessity of many months of treatment, the increase in HIV-associated tuberculosis, and the emergence of drug-resistant cases in the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_16-20"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>In developed countries, tuberculosis is less common and is found mainly in urban areas. In Europe, deaths from TB fell from 500 out of 100,000 in 1850 to 50 out of 100,000 by 1950. Improvements in public health were reducing tuberculosis even before the arrival of antibiotics, although the disease remained a significant threat to public health, such that when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_Research_Council_(UK)" title="Medical Research Council (UK)">Medical Research Council</a> was formed in Britain in 1913 its initial focus was tuberculosis research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2010, rates per 100,000 people in different areas of the world were: globally 178, Africa 332, the Americas 36, Eastern Mediterranean 173, Europe 63, Southeast Asia 278, and Western Pacific 139.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011Control_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011Control-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Russia">Russia</span></h4>
<p>Russia has achieved particularly dramatic progress with a decline in its TB mortality rate—from 61.9 per 100,000 in 1965 to 2.7 per 100,000 in 1993;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011a_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011a-148">[148]</a></sup> however, mortality rate increased to 24 per 100,000 in 2005 and then recoiled to 11 per 100,000 by 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span></h4>
<p>China has achieved particularly dramatic progress, with about an 80% reduction in its TB mortality rate between 1990 and 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011Control_133-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011Control-133">[133]</a></sup> The number of new cases has declined by 17% between 2004 and 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EIU_2014_120-3"><a href="#cite_note-EIU_2014-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span></h4>
<p>In 2007, the country with the highest estimated incidence rate of TB was <a href="/wiki/Eswatini" title="Eswatini">Eswatini</a>, with 1,200 cases per 100,000 people. In 2017, the country with the highest estimated <a href="/wiki/Incidence_(epidemiology)" title="Incidence (epidemiology)">incidence rate</a> as a % of the population was <a href="/wiki/Lesotho" title="Lesotho">Lesotho</a>, with 665 cases per 100,000 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_tuberculosis_report_2018_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Global_tuberculosis_report_2018-150">[150]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span></h4>
<p>As of 2017, India had the largest total incidence, with an estimated 2,740,000 cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_tuberculosis_report_2018_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-Global_tuberculosis_report_2018-150">[150]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO), in 2000–2015, India's estimated mortality rate dropped from 55 to 36 per 100,000 population per year with estimated 480 thousand people died of TB in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> In India a major proportion of tuberculosis patients are being treated by private partners and private hospitals. Evidence indicates that the tuberculosis national survey does not represent the number of cases that are diagnosed and recorded by private clinics and hospitals in India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span></h4>
<p>In the United States <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans</a> have a fivefold greater mortality from TB,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> and racial and ethnic minorities accounted for 84% of all reported TB cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, the overall tuberculosis case rate was 3 per 100,000 persons in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_tuberculosis_report_2018_150-2"><a href="#cite_note-Global_tuberculosis_report_2018-150">[150]</a></sup> In Canada, tuberculosis is still endemic in some rural areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_Europe">Western Europe</span></h4>
<p>In 2017, in the United Kingdom, the national average was 9 per 100,000 and the highest incidence rates in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> were 20 per 100,000 in Portugal.
</p>
